## Remarks on some Limit Geometric Properties related to an Idempotent and Non-Associative Algebraic Structure

∗ Walter Briec

April 8, 2025

## Abstract

This article analyzes the geometric properties of an idempotent, non-associative algebraic structure that extends the Max-Times semiring. This algebraic structure is useful for studying systems of MaxTimes and Max-Plus equations, employing an appropriate notion of a non-associative determinant. We consider a connected ultrametric distance and demonstrate that it implies, among other properties, an analogue of the Pythagorean relation. To this end, we introduce a suitable notion of a right angle between two vectors and investigate a trigonometric concept associated with the Chebyshev unit ball. Following this approach, we explore the potential implications of these properties in the complex plane.

We provide an algebraic definition of a line passing through two points, which corresponds to the Painlev´ e-Peano-Kuratowski limit of a sequence of generalized lines. We establish that this definition leads to distinctive geometric properties; in particular, two distinct parallel lines may share an infinite number of points.

AMS:

06D50, 32F17

Keywords: Generalized mean, convexity, convex hull, ultrametric, semilattice, B -convexity.

## Introduction

It is well known that algebras associated with idempotent semirings are related to particular geometric representations. This article investigates some of these properties through the non-associative symmetrization of Max-Times algebras. The concept of B -convexity was introduced in [12] as a Painlev´ e-Kuratowski limit of generalized convexities. 1 This fundamental idea is related to earlier work by Ben-Tal [5] and Avriel [2]. When restricted to the nonnegative orthant, B -convexity coincides with Max-Times convexity. In this context, an algebraic formulation has been established in the idempotent Max-Times semiring ( R + , max , × ) by applying the transformation + → max in the nonnegative Euclidean orthant. The idempotent Max-Times semiring is homeomorphic to the Max-Plus semiring ( R ∪{-∞} , max , +) via a logarithmic transformation, and B -convex sets emerge from a broad class of idempotent algebraic structures introduced in [26] and derived from the notion of dequantization (see also [24]).

Hahn-Banach-type separation properties [14] and fixed-point results have been established in [13]. Other idempotent convex structures have been proposed in [1] and [30]. The algebraic formulation of B -convexity was previously limited to the Max-Times semi-module ( R n + , ∨ , × ). To address this limitation, an idempotent and non-associative binary operation was introduced in [8] by considering a special class of idempotent and non-associative magmas. In this paper, we introduce an n -ary operation associated with a suitable class of

∗ University of Perpignan, LAMPS, 52 avenue Villeneuve, 66000 Perpignan, France. emailbriec@univ-perp.fr

1 The letter B , used to define a corresponding notion of convexity may be confusing in light of the concept of convexity introduced by Anatole Beck in Banach spaces in 1962. It was chosen due to the origins of the names of several members of the mathematics department at the University of Perpignan at the time of the publication of the first paper [12]. Additionally, it can be linked to the work of Aaron Ben-Tal [5], whose paper plays a crucial role in the context of these articles. Another reason for this choice is that the operation /squareplus , pronounced ' B oxplus,' bears a graphical resemblance to the Chebyshev unit ball ( /lscript ∞ ) when represented in two dimensions with Cartesian coordinates. This ball plays a central role in the proposed approach and is itself the Hausdorff limit of the sequence of /lscript p balls as p →∞ .

regularized semicontinuous operators. It is worth noting that, when involving only two points, the idempotent and non-associative algebraic structures considered are also mentioned by Gaubert [16] and discussed Viro [31], who explored the complex case.

Note that there exist other approaches to extending idempotent algebraic structures. For example, Izhakian and Rowen [19] introduced supertropical algebra, which involves a similar type of construction based on the notion of a valued monoid. Additionally, the concept of hyperfields, introduced in [21] and [25], provides an alternative approach.

Relaxing associativity preserves both symmetry and idempotency. It has been shown that this notion of convexity can be equivalently characterized by the Kuratowski-Painlev´ e limit of the generalized convex hull of two points as defined in [12]. Some separation results in R n have been established in [9], and the limiting structure of polytopes has been analyzed in [10].

More recently, these structures have been examined from an algebraic perspective to compute the limit solutions of systems of Max-Times equations, particularly by introducing a special determinant formulation. These results also have geometric implications for determining the equation of a limit hyperplane passing through n points, with possible extensions to Max-Plus algebra.

This paper continues prior research on this topic from a geometric perspective, introducing appropriate notions of distance. Throughout the paper, an idempotent analogue of subtraction plays a key role.

First, we define a space notion suited to the algebraic structure under consideration and generalize the definition of B -space introduced in [13]. Second, we construct a sequence of generalized metrics via an appropriate homeomorphic transformation of the vector space structure. We demonstrate that their limit properties yield an ultrametric distance intimately linked to the algebraic structure considered. To this end, we derive a notion of orthogonality from the limit inner product defined in [8]. As a result, we establish geometric properties of a special class of right-angle triples, specifically proving a Pythagorean-type relation within this framework. Using the limiting properties of the generalized inner product, we construct a pseudocosine limit function, where the pseudo-cosine is derived from the limiting inner product, and the pseudo-sine stems from the two-dimensional determinant formula established in [11]. Following this approach, we explore potential implications of these properties in the complex plane.

Finally, the paper investigates a notion of line corresponding to the non-associative algebraic structure under study. We show that the Painlev´ e-Kuratowski limit of a sequence of generalized lines admits an algebraic description extending a previous result in [8] for a line segment. We establish a condition ensuring that two boundary lines are parallel, analogous (in a certain sense) to the criterion for parallel lines in a vector space. The paper concludes with an analysis of the two-dimensional case, in which a special idempotent geometry is examined. Building on [11], we demonstrate how to compute the equation of a two-dimensional line. The final result describes a plane geometry where a unique line passes through two distinct points, and where a line segment can be extended to a half-line. However, two distinct parallel lines may share an infinite number of points.

The paper is structured as follows. Section 1 introduces the framework of the non-associative idempotent algebraic structure defined in [8], along with some associated convexity notions. Section 2 defines field and space concepts related to this algebraic structure, providing illustrative examples. Section 3 derives an ultrametric distance as the limit of a sequence of generalized metrics. In this context, a triangular equality is established for a special class of right-angle triples. Section 4 explores inner product properties established in [9] and introduces a related notion of pseudo-cosine. Section 5 presents an algebraic description of a line. The paper concludes with an analysis of the geometric implications of the considered algebraic structure.

## 1 Idempotent and Non-Associative Algebraic and Convex Structure

## 1.1 Isomorphism of vector-Space Structures in Limit

For all p ∈ N , let us consider a bijection ϕ p : R -→ R defined by:

$$\varphi _ { p } \colon \lambda \longrightarrow \lambda ^ { 2 p + 1 }$$

and φ p ( x 1 , ..., x n ) = ( ϕ p ( x 1 ) , ..., ϕ p ( x n )). This is closely related to the approach proposed by Ben-Tal [5] and Avriel [2]. One can induce a field structure on R for which ϕ p becomes a field isomorphism. For λ, µ ∈ R , the indexed sum and the indexed product are simply given by and

$$\lambda ^ { \ p } _ { + } & = \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \varphi _ { p } ( \lambda ) + \varphi _ { p } ( \mu ) ) = ( \lambda ^ { 2 p + 1 } + \mu ^ { 2 p + 1 } ) ^ { \frac { 1 } { 2 p + 1 } } , \\ & \\ & \\ & , \ p$$

These two operations define a scalar field ( R , p + , · ). Given this change of notation via ϕ p and φ p , we can define a R -vector space structure on R n by: λ ϕ p · x = φ -1 p ( ϕ p ( λ ) · φ p ( x ) ) = λ · x and x ϕ p + y = φ -1 p ( φ p ( x ) + φ p ( y ) ) . We call these two operations the multiplication by scalar and the indexed sum (indexed by ϕ p ).

$$\lambda \cdot \mu = \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \varphi _ { p } ( \lambda ) \cdot \varphi _ { p } ( \mu ) ) = \lambda \cdot \mu . \\ \intertext { f i n e a s c a l r e f i d e { \left ( \mathbb { R } , \stackrel { p } { + } , \cdot \right ) } . \text { Given this change of notation via } \varphi _ { p } \text { and } \phi _ { p } , w e c a n d f i n e }$$

The ϕ p -sum denoted ϕ p ∑ of ( x (1) , ..., x ( m ) ) ∈ R n × m is defined by 2

$$\sum _ { j \in [ m ] } ^ { \varphi _ { p } } x ^ { ( j ) } = \phi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } \left ( \sum _ { j \in [ m ] } \phi _ { p } ( x ^ { ( j ) } ) \right ) . \\$$

For the sake simplicity, throughout the paper we denote for all x, y ∈ R n :

$$x + y = x + \stackrel { p } { y } .$$

Recall that Kuratowski-Painlev´ e lower limit of the sequence of sets { A n } n ∈ N , denoted Li n →∞ A n , is the set of points x for which there exists a sequence { x ( n ) } n ∈ N of points such that x ( n ) ∈ A n for all n and x = lim n →∞ x ( n ) . The Kuratowski-Painlev´ e upper limit of the sequence of sets { A n } n ∈ N , denoted Ls n →∞ A n , is the set of points x for which there exists a subsequence { x ( n k ) } k ∈ N of points such that x ( n k ) ∈ A n k for all k and x = lim k →∞ x ( n k ) . A sequence { A n } n ∈ N of subsets of R n is said to converge, in the Kuratowski-Painlev´ e sense, to a set A if Ls n →∞ A n = A = Li n →∞ A n , in which case we write A = Lim n →∞ A n .

Along this line one can introduce the binary operation /squareplus defined for all λ, µ ∈ R by:

$$\text {shown that for all } X , \mu \in \mathbb { E } \text { we have.} \\ \lim _ { p \longrightarrow + \infty } \lambda + \mu = \begin{cases} \frac { p } { 2 } ( \lambda + \mu ) & \text {if } \ | \lambda | > \ | \mu | \\ \mu & \text {if } \ | \lambda | < \ | \mu | . \end{cases}$$

$$\lambda \oplus \mu = \lim _ { p \rightarrow + \infty } \lambda \stackrel { p } { + } \mu .$$

Though the operation /squareplus does not satisfy associativity, it can be extended by constructing a non-associative algebraic structure which returns to a given n -tuple a real value. For all x ∈ R n and all subsets I of [ n ], let us consider the map ξ I [ x ] : R -→ Z defined for all α ∈ R by

$$\xi _ { I } [ x ] ( \alpha ) = C \text {and} \{ i \in I \colon x _ { i } = \alpha \} - C \text {card} \{ i \in I \colon x _ { i } = - \alpha \} .$$

This map measures the symmetry of the occurrences of a given value α in the coordinates of a vector x . R n

$$\prod _ { i \in \{ 1 , 2 \} } x _ { i } = x _ { 1 } \, \mathbb { E } \, x _ { 2 } .$$

There are some basic properties that can be inherited from the above algebraic structure: ( i ) for all α ∈ R , one has: α ( /squareplus i ∈ I x i ) = /squareplus i ∈ I ( αx i ); ( ii ) suppose that x ∈ /epsilon1 R n + where /epsilon1 is +1 or -1. Then /squareplus i ∈ I x i = /epsilon1 max i ∈ I { /epsilon1x i } ; ( iii ) we have | /squareplus i ∈ I x i | ≤ /squareplus i ∈ I | x i | ; ( iv ) for all x ∈ R n :

The algebraic structure ( R , /squareplus , · ) can be extended to R n . Suppose that x, y ∈ R n , and let us denote x /squareplus y = ( x 1 /squareplus y 1 , ..., x n /squareplus y n ) . Moreover, let us consider m vectors x (1) , ..., x ( m ) ∈ R n , and define

$$\prod _ { j \in [ m ] } x ^ { ( j ) } & = \left ( \prod _ { j \in [ m ] } x _ { 1 } ^ { ( j ) } , \dots , \prod _ { j \in [ m ] } x _ { n } ^ { ( j ) } \right ) . \\$$

The n -ary operation ( x 1 , ..., x n ) → /squareplus i ∈ [ n ] x i is not associative. To simplify the notations of the paper, for all z ∈ { z i 1 ,...,i m : i k ∈ I k , k ∈ [ m ] } , where I 1 , ..., I m are m index subsets of N , we use the notation:

$$\prod _ { \substack { i _ { k } \in I _ { k } \\ k \in [ m ] } } z _ { i _ { 1 } , \dots , i _ { n } } = \prod _ { ( i _ { 1 } , \dots , i _ { m } ) \in \prod _ { k \in [ m ] } I _ { k } } z _ { i _ { 1 } , \dots , i _ { m } } .$$

Notice that since the operation /squareplus is not associative, it can be ambiguous to apply without using the symbol /squareplus indexed on a given finite subset I . In the remainder, for the sake of simplicity, and when it is more convenient, we will adopt the following notational convention. For all x ∈ R n :

$$\coprod _ { i \in [ n ] } x _ { i } = x _ { i } \, \mathbb { I } \, \mathbb { \cdots } \, \mathbb { I } \, x _ { n } .$$

In the sequel, for all x, y ∈ R n we will often use the following notation to define an idempotent analogue of the subtraction operation:

$$x \, \Box \, y = x \, \mathbb { E } \left ( - y \right ) .$$

## 1.2 Scalar Products and Determinants in Limit

This section presents the algebraic properties induced by an isomorphism between a scalar field and a set and their implications on the scalar product. If 〈· , ·〉 is the canonical inner product over R n , then there exists a symmetric bilinear form 〈· , ·〉 ϕ p : R n × R n -→ R defined by:

$$\langle x , y \rangle _ { \varphi _ { p } } = \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \langle \phi _ { p } ( x ) , \phi _ { p } ( y ) \rangle ) = ( \sum _ { i \in [ n ] } x _ { i } ^ { 2 p + 1 } y _ { i } ^ { 2 p + 1 } ) ^ { \frac { 1 } { 2 p + 1 } } .$$

Now, let us denote [ 〈 y, ·〉 ϕ p ≤ λ ] = { x ∈ R n : 〈 y, x 〉 ϕ p ≤ λ } . For the sake of simplicity, let us denote 〈· , ·〉 p this scalar product.

In what follows, we state some results obtained in [8, 9] by taking the limit. We first introduce the operation 〈· , ·〉 ∞ : R n × R n -→ R defined for all x, y ∈ R n by 〈 x, y 〉 ∞ = /squareplus i ∈ [ n ] x i y i . Let ‖ · ‖ ∞ be the Tchebychev norm defined by ‖ x ‖ ∞ = max i ∈ [ n ] | x i | . It is established in [8] that for all x, y ∈ R n , we have: ( i ) √ 〈 x, x 〉 ∞ = ‖ x ‖ ∞ ; ( ii ) |〈 x, y 〉 ∞ | ≤ ‖ x ‖ ∞ ‖ y ‖ ∞ ; ( iii ) for all α ∈ R , α 〈 x, y 〉 ∞ = 〈 αx, y 〉 ∞ = 〈 x, αy 〉 ∞ . By definition, we have for all x, y ∈ R n :

$$\langle x , y \rangle _ { \infty } = \lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } \langle x , y \rangle _ { p } .$$

A map f : R n -→ R defined as f ( x ) = 〈 a, x 〉 ∞ for some a ∈ R n is called an idempotent symmetric form . In the following, for all maps f : R n -→ R and all real numbers c , the notation [ f ≤ c ] stands for the set f -1 ( ] -∞ , c ] ). Similarly, [ f &lt; c ] stands for f -1 ( ] -∞ , c [ ) and [ f ≥ c ] = [ -f ≤ -c ]. For all u, v ∈ R , let us define the binary operation

Note that in [19], a polynomial theory for supertropical algebra was proposed. To achieve this, a similar operation was constructed by extending the tropical semiring with a suitable valuation function. Both proposed constructions follow a similar principle. However, in our approach, this binary operation is derived from the idempotent semiring ( R + , max , · ) and the absolute value function.

$$\begin{array} { r } { \frac { u \, \bar { \ } v = \begin{cases} u & i f \quad | u | \ > \ | v | \\ v & i f \quad | u | \ < \ | v | . } \\ \end{cases} } \\ { o m i a l l \, t h e r o y \, f o r \, s u p t r o p i c a l \, a g e b r a \, w a s \, p o s e d . } \\ { d \, b y \, e x t e n d i n g \, t h e \, t r o p i c a l \, s e m i r i n g \, w i t h \, a \, s u i t b l e } \end{array}$$

An elementary calculus shows that u /squareplus v = 1 2 [ u -/slurbelow v -[ ( -u ) -/slurbelow ( -v ) ] ] . It was shown in [14], that this operation is associative.

Given m elements u 1 , ..., u m of R , not all of which are 0, let I + , respectively I -, be the set of indices for which 0 &lt; u i , respectively u i &lt; 0. We can then write u 1 -/slurbelow · · · -/slurbelow u m = ( -/slurbelow i ∈ I + u i ) -/slurbelow ( -/slurbelow i ∈ I -u i ) = (max i ∈ I + u i ) -/slurbelow (min i ∈ I -u i ) from which we have

$$( \max _ { i } \in I _ { + } + u _ { i } ) \subseteq ( \min _ { i } \in I _ { - } \ u _ { i } ) \text { from which we have} \\ u _ { 1 } \subseteq \dots \subseteq u _ { m } = \left \{ \begin{array} { l l l } \max _ { i \in I _ { + } } u _ { i } & i \text { } I _ { - } = \emptyset \text { or } \max _ { i \in I _ { - } } | u _ { i } | < \max _ { i \in I _ { + } } | u _ { i } \\ \min _ { i \in I _ { - } } u _ { i } & i \text { } I _ { + } = \emptyset \text { or } \max _ { i \in I _ { + } } | u _ { i } | < \max _ { i \in I _ { - } } | u _ { i } | \\ \min _ { i \in I _ { - } } u _ { i } & i \text { } \quad \max _ { i \in I _ { - } } | u _ { i } | = \max _ { i \in I _ { + } } | u _ { i } . \\ \end{array}$$

$$g ( x _ { 1 } , \dots , x _ { n } ) = \langle a , x \rangle _ { \infty } ^ { - } = a _ { 1 } x _ { 1 } \, \underbar { \cup } \, \dots \, \underbar { \cup } \, a _ { n } x _ { n } . \\ \intertext { l i n $ [ 0 ] $ t h t f o r $ e v . $ l l $ o c $ T $ r $ b $ h $ t h t $ o t $ e $ - 1 } \, \intertext { i n $ [ 0 ] $ t h t f o r $ e v . $ l l $ o c $ T $ r $ b $ h $ t h t $ o t $ e $ - 1 }$$

We define an idempotent lower symmetric form on R n as a map g : R n → R such that for all ( x 1 , ..., x n ) ∈ R n ,

It was established in [8] that for all c ∈ R , the set g -1 ( ] -∞ , c ]) = { x ∈ R n | g ( x ) ≤ c } is closed. It follows that an idempotent lower symmetric form is lower semi-continuous. It was also shown in [8] that for any a ∈ R n , the idempotent lower symmetrical form g defined by g ( x 1 , ..., x n ) = a 1 x 1 -/slurbelow · · · -/slurbelow a n x n , is the lower semi-continuous regularization of the map x ↦→〈 a, x 〉 ∞ = /squareplus i ∈ [ n ] a i x i .

$$u \stackrel { + } { \sim } v = - [ ( - u ) \smile ( - v ) ] , \\ \text {these lines, one can define an idempotent upper symmetric form as a map} \\$$

Similarly, one can introduce a binary operation defined for all u, v ∈ R as which is associative. Along these lines, one can define an idempotent upper symmetric form as a map h : R n → R such that, for all ( x 1 , ..., x n ) ∈ R n ,

$$h ( x _ { 1 } , \dots , x _ { n } ) = \langle a , x \rangle _ { \infty } ^ { + } = a _ { 1 } x _ { 1 } \underbrace { + } _ { } \dots \underbrace { + } _ { } a _ { n } x _ { n } . \\$$

For all x ∈ R n , we clearly, have the following identities

$$\langle a , x \rangle _ { \infty } ^ { + } = - \langle a , - x \rangle _ { \infty } ^ { - } \text { and } \langle a , x \rangle _ { \infty } ^ { - } = - \langle a , - x \rangle _ { \infty } ^ { + } .$$

For any a ∈ R n , the idempotent upper symmetric form h defined by h ( x 1 , ..., x n ) = a 1 x 1 + /slurbelow · · · + /slurbelow a n x n , , is the upper semi-continuous regularization of the map x ↦→〈 a, x 〉 ∞ = /squareplus i ∈ [ n ] a i x i .

These forms can be related to the construction of a ring involving a balance relation symmetrizing the semi-ring ( R + , ∨ , · ) (see [11], see also [18] and [28] in a Max-Plus context).

More, recently, the following notion of determinant was introduced in [11]. The set R , equipped with the operation /squareplus , allows for the definition of a suitable notion of determinant (see [10, 11]). We denote by M n ( R ) the set of square matrices of order n with real entries. We introduce over M n ( R ) the map Φ p : M n ( R ) -→ M n ( R ) defined for all matrices A = ( a i,j ) i =1 ...n j =1 ..n ∈ M n ( R ) by:

$$\Phi _ { p } ( A ) = \left ( \varphi _ { p } ( a _ { i , j } ) \right ) _ { j = 1 \dots n } = ( a _ { i , j } \, ^ { 2 p + 1 } ) _ { j = 1 \dots n } \, . \\ \intertext { a n d } \Phi _ { p } - 1 \, \colon \mathcal { M } _ { n } ( \mathbb { R } ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { n } ( \mathbb { R } ) \, \text { defined by} \, .$$

$$\Phi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( A ) = \left ( \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( a _ { i , j } ) \right ) _ { j = 1 \dots n } = \left ( a _ { i , j } \frac { \frac { 1 } { 2 p + 1 } } { 2 ^ { 1 + p } } \right ) _ { j = 1 \dots n } . \\ \intertext { s t e n s } \text {extension of the man } \phi _ { n } \text { from } \mathbb { R } ^ { n } \text { to } M _ { n } ( \mathbb { R } ) \text { \ } \Phi _ { n } ( A ) \text { ;} \text { this the } 2 n + 1 \text { Hadamard lower of matrix }$$

Its reciprocal is the map Φ -1 p : M n ( R ) -→ M n ( R ) defined by:

Φ p is a natural extension of the map φ p from R n to M n ( R ). Φ p ( A ) is the 2 p +1 Hadamard power of matrix A . In the following we introduce the matrix product:

$$A ^ { \ p } \cdot x & = \phi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \Phi _ { p } ( A ) . \phi _ { p } ( x ) ) . \\ \intertext { a n p } \exp { x } & \mapsto A ^ { \ p } \cdot x \text { is } \varphi _ { p } \text {-linear} , \text { that is linear with respect to the algebraic operation}$$

For any n × n matrix A , let | A | denote its determinant. We now introduce the following definition of the ϕ p -determinant:

It is easy to see that the map x ↦→ A p · x is ϕ p -linear, that is linear with respect to the algebraic operation p + and the usual scalar multiplication. Conversely, if g is a ϕ p -linear map then it can be represented by a matrix A such that g ( x ) = A p · x for all x ∈ R n .

$$| A | _ { p } = \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 1 } | \Phi _ { p } ( A ) | .$$

$$| A | _ { p } = \left ( \sum _ { \sigma \in \mathfrak { S } _ { n } } s g n ( \sigma ) \prod _ { i \in [ n ] } a _ { i , \sigma ( i ) } ^ { 2 p + 1 } \right ) ^ { \frac { 1 } { 2 p + 1 } } ,$$

where S n is the set of all the permutations defined on [ n ].

Along this line, one can define a determinant in limit as follows:

$$\lim _ { p \to \infty } | A | _ { p } \coloneqq | A | _ { \infty } & = \bigoplus _ { \sigma \in \mathfrak { S } _ { n } } \left ( \ s g n ( \sigma ) \prod _ { i \in [ n ] } a _ { i , \sigma ( i ) } \right ) . \\$$

This notion is compared in [11] to that of permanent originally defined in the framework of Max-Plus algebras (se [7] and [6]).

## 1.3 An Idempotent and Non-associative Convex Structure

In [12], B -convexity is introduced as a limit of linear convexities. The φ p -convex hull of a finite set A = { x (1) , ..., x ( m ) } ⊂ R n is defined by:

$$C o ^ { \phi _ { p } } ( A ) = \left \{ \sum _ { i \in [ m ] } t _ { i } \stackrel { \varphi _ { p } } { \cdot } x ^ { ( i ) } \colon \sum _ { i \in [ m ] } t _ { i } = \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( 1 ) , \varphi _ { p } ( t _ { i } ) \geq 0 , i \in [ m ] \right \}$$

which can be rewritten using the fact that ϕ -1 p (1) = 1 and ϕ p is increasing:

$$C o ^ { \phi _ { p } } ( A ) = \left \{ \phi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } \left ( \sum _ { i \in [ m ] } t _ { i } ^ { 2 p + 1 } \cdot \phi _ { p } ( x ^ { ( i ) } ) \right ) \colon ( \sum _ { i \in [ m ] } t _ { i } ^ { 2 p + 1 } ) ^ { \frac { 1 } { 2 p + 1 } } = 1 , t _ { i } \geq 0 , i \in [ m ] \right \} .$$

Using the Leibnitz formula yields

Moreover, for all L ⊂ R , we simplify the notations denoting Co p ( L ) = Co φ p ( L ). The definition of B -convex sets is based on the definition of the Kuratowski-Painlev´ e limit of a sequence of φ p -convex sets. For a non-empty finite subset A ⊂ R n we let Co ∞ ( A ) = Ls p -→∞ Co p ( A ). A subset C of R n is B -convex if for all finite subsets A of C , Co ∞ ( A ) ⊂ C . The structure of B -convex sets in R n + will involve the order structure, with respect to the positive cone of R n ; we denote by ∨ i ∈ [ m ] x ( i ) the least upper bound of x (1) , ..., x ( m ) ∈ R n , that is:

$$\bigvee _ { i \in [ m ] } x ^ { ( i ) } = \left ( \max \{ x _ { 1 } ^ { ( 1 ) } , \dots , x _ { 1 } ^ { ( m ) } \} , \dots , \max \{ x _ { n } ^ { ( 1 ) } , \dots , x _ { n } ^ { ( m ) } \} \right ) \\$$

When A = { x (1) , ..., x ( m ) } ⊂ R n + , it was shown in [12] that:

$$C o ^ { \infty } ( A ) = & \{ \ \vee _ { i \in [ m ] } t _ { i } x ^ { ( i ) } \colon t _ { i } \in [ 0 , 1 ] , \max _ { i \in [ m ] } t _ { i } = 1 \} = \lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } C o ^ { p } ( A ) . \\ \cdots & \quad \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ Moreover it is also proved that the sequence is Hausdorff convergent. In addition, if the set A is contained in any n -dimensional orthant, it was established in [8] that Co ∞ ( A ) = { /squareplus i ∈ [ m ] t i x ( i ) : t i ∈ [0 , 1] , max i ∈ [ m ] t i = 1 } .

An important limitation of this definition of B -convexity is that its algebraic form is restricted to the non-negative orthant R n + , over which the idempotent Max-Times semiring is considered. To circumvent this difficulty an extended definition of B -convexity other R n was suggested in [8]. A subset C of R n is said to be idempotent symmetric convex 3 if, for all x, y ∈ C and for all t ∈ [0 , 1],

If L ⊂ R n + is B -convex, then L = Lim p -→∞ Co p ( L ) = ⋂ ∞ r =0 Co p ( L ). For all S ⊂ R n + , we denote Co ∞ ( S ) = Lim p -→∞ Co p ( S ). Properties of this operation are developed in [12]. In particular, for all S ⊂ R n + , Co ∞ ( S ) is B -convex or equivalently Max-Times convex.

$$x \oplus t y \in C$$

It was shown in [8] that the sequence of subsets { Co ( p ) ( x, y ) } p ∈ N of R n has a Painlev´ e-Kuratowski limit denoted Co ∞ ( x, y ). These points can be expressed trough an explicit algebraic formula in term of the idempotent and non-associative algebraic structure defined on ( R n , /squareplus , · ).

Moreover, it was established in [8] that this limit entirely characterizes an idempotent symmetric convex set. A subset C of R n is idempotent symmetric convex if and only if for all x, y ∈ R n Co ∞ ( x, y ) ⊂ C . More recently, it has been established in [10] that for all x, y ∈ R n , Co ∞ ( x, y ) is idempotent symmetric convex and thereby Co ∞ ( x, y ) is the smallest idempotent symmetric convex set that contains { x, y } .

3 Referred to as B /sharp -convexity in [8], this concept is given a more intuitive terminology here.

## 2 Idempotent Symmetric and Ultrametric Spaces

The concept of B -space, as introduced in [14], is restricted to the non-negative n -dimensional orthant R n + . In this work, we extend it to the entire Euclidean vector space. This new definition will be useful for analyzing the algebraic structure of a line defined on ( R n , /squareplus , · ).

## 2.1 Idempotent Symmetric Fields and Spaces

A finite-dimensional B -space (Max-Times space) is, by definition, a subset X of R n + such that 0 ∈ X , for all t ≥ 0 and all x ∈ X , tx ∈ X and for all x, y ∈ X , x ∨ ty ∈ X .

We first define a suitable notion of idempotent pseudo-field . By comparing this definition with that of a field, note that we consider a weakened form of associativity and idempotence is added.

Definition 2.1.1 A triple ( K, /squareplus K , · K ) is an idempotent pseudo-field if:

- of associativity).

( a ) There exits a binary operation /squareplus K : K × K -→ K that satisfies the following properties: ( i ) for all λ ∈ K we have λ /squareplus K λ = λ (idempotence); ( ii ) for all ( λ, µ ) ∈ K × K we have λ /squareplus K µ = µ /squareplus K λ (commutativity); ( iii ) there exists a neutral element 0 K ∈ K such that λ /squareplus K 0 K = 0 K /squareplus K λ = λ for all λ ∈ K ; ( iv ) every λ ∈ K has a symmetric element -λ such that ( -λ ) /squareplus K λ = λ /squareplus K ( -λ ) = 0 X (symmetry); ( v ) for any mutually non-symmetric triple of elements ( λ, µ, η ) ∈ K 3 , ( λ /squareplus K µ ) /squareplus K η = λ /squareplus K ( µ /squareplus K η ) (weakened form

- ( b ) There exists a scalar multiplication · K : K × K -→ K that satisfies the following properties: ( i ) for all λ ∈ K and all ( λ, µ ) ∈ K × K , we have λ · K µ = µ · K λ (commutativity); ( ii ) for all λ, µ, η ∈ K × K × K we have ( λ · K µ ) · K η = λ · K ( µ · K η ) (associativity); ( iii ) there is an element 1 K ∈ K such that for all λ ∈ K we have 1 K · K λ = λ ; ( iv ) for all λ ∈ K \{ 0 K } there exists an element λ -1 such that λ · K λ -1 = 1 K .
- ( c ) The multiplication · K is distributive over the operation /squareplus K : for all λ ∈ K and all ( µ, ν ) ∈ K × K , we have

$$\lambda \cdot _ { K } ( \mu \, \mathbb { E } _ { K } \, \nu ) = ( \lambda \cdot _ { K } \mu ) \, \mathbb { E } _ { K } \, ( \lambda \cdot _ { K } \nu ) .$$

Example 2.1.2 From [8] , the triple ( R , /squareplus , · ) , where /squareplus is the binary operation defined in (6) is an idempotent pseudo-field.

In the following, when we use the /squareplus symbol without further specification on R n this will mean that we are considering the binary operation defined in equation (6) and its related n -ary extension.

Example 2.1.3 We consider the case of the non-associative symmetrisation of the Max-Plus semi-module analysed in [11]. Let M = R ∪ {-∞} and let us denote ( M , ⊕ , ⊗ ) the Maslov's semi-module where we replace the operations ∨ with ⊕ and + with ⊗ . More precisely one can define on M the operations ⊕ and ⊗ respectively as x ⊕ y = max { x, y } and x ⊗ y = x + y , where -∞ is the neutral element of the operation ⊕ .

The formalism proposed in this paper is however convenient for introducing the following extended logarithmic function to ˜ M and transferring the algebraic structure of the real set. Let ψ ln : R -→ ˜ M be the map defined by:

The map x ↦→ ψ ln ( x ) is an isomorphism from R to ˜ M . Let ψ exp ( x ) : ˜ M -→ R be its inverse. Notice that ψ ln ( -1) = i π . By definition we have z ˜ /squareplus u = ψ ln ( ψ exp ( z ) /squareplus ψ exp ( u ) ) . Moreover, we have z ˜ ⊗ u = ψ ln ( ψ exp ( z ) ⊗ ψ exp ( u ) ) . It follows that ( ˜ M , ˜ ⊕ , ˜ ⊗ ) is isomorphic to ( R , /squareplus , · ) and is also an idempotent pseudofield with the neutral elements 0 ˜ M = -∞ and 1 ˜ M = 0 .

Remark 2.1.5 In [31] , an idempotent algebraic structure was derived from a suitable dequantization principle defined over the complex scalar field. Let us consider the binary operation /squareplus C : C × C -→ C defined as:

It is easy to check that this operation is idempotent, has 0 as neutral element and is symmetric. Moreover, the standard product of complex numbers fulfils all the desired conditions. In addition note that this product is distributive over the operation /squareplus C . In particular, if | z | = | w | = ρ &gt; 0 then we have for all u ∈ C :

$$\begin{array} { c } \text {the complex scalar field.} \quad \text {let us consider the binary operation } \mathbb { E } _ { C } \colon \mathbb { C } \times \mathbb { C } \longrightarrow \mathbb { C } \text { defined} \\ \\ z \oplus \mathbb { E } _ { C } w = \begin{cases} z & \text {if} \quad | z | > | w | \\ w & \text {if} \quad | z | < | w | \\ 0 & \text {if} \quad z + w = 0 \\ \rho _ { | w | + w } ^ { \frac { 2 } { 2 } + w } & \text {if} \quad | z | = | w | = \rho > 0 . \\ \end{cases} \\ \intertext { t h a t h s i n d e r $ i s $ i n d e r $ i n d e r $ i s $ a n e u r t a l $ } \text {of complex numbers full} \quad \text {the desired conditions.} \ \text {in addition note that this product}$$

$$u \cdot ( z \, \mathbb { E } _ { \mathrm C } \, w ) = u \rho \frac { z + w } { | z + w | } = \rho \frac { u z + u w } { | z + w | } = \rho | u | \frac { u z + u w } { | u z + u w | } = ( u z ) \, \mathbb { E } _ { \mathrm C } \, ( u w ) . \\$$

However, it does not satisfy the weakened form of associativity of an idempotent pseudo-field.

Definition 2.1.6 A triple ( X, /squareplus X , · K ) is an idempotent symmetric space defined over a idempotent pseudofield K if:

( a ) There exits a binary operation /squareplus X : X × X -→ X that satisfies the following properties: ( i ) for all x ∈ X we have x /squareplus X x = x ; ( ii ) for all ( x, y ) ∈ X × X we have x /squareplus X y = y /squareplus X x ; ( iii ) there exists a neutral element 0 X ∈ X such that x /squareplus X 0 X = 0 X /squareplus X x for all x ∈ X ; ( iv ) every x ∈ X has a symmetric element -x such that ( -x ) /squareplus X x = x /squareplus X ( -x ) = 0 X .

( b ) There exists a scalar multiplication · K : R × X -→ X that satisfies the following properties: ( i ) for all λ ∈ R and all ( x, y ) ∈ X × X , we have λ · K ( x /squareplus X y ) = ( λ · K x ) /squareplus X ( λ · K y ) ; ( ii ) for all λ, µ ∈ R × R we have ( λ /squareplus K µ ) · K x = ( λ · K x ) /squareplus X ( µ · K x ) , ( iii ) for all λ, µ ∈ R × R we have ( λ · K µ ) · K x = λ · K ( µ · K x ) ; ( iv ) for all x ∈ X we have 1 · K x = x and -x = ( -1) · K x .

Suppose that X is an idempotent symmetric space. A subset Y of X is an idempotent symmetric subspace if for all x, y ∈ Y and all t ∈ R , x /squareplus X ty ∈ Y . Notice that, in this definition, associativity has been replaced with idempotence. Clearly, ( R , /squareplus , · ) is an idempotent symmetric space.

Lemma 2.1.7 Suppose that ( X, /squareplus X , · K ) is an idempotent symmetric space defined over an idempotent pseudo-field K . Then, for all x ∈ X , one has:

- ( b ) 0 K · K x = 0 X
- ( a ) λ · K 0 X = 0 X

Proof: ( a ) By definition, for all x ∈ X and all λ ∈ K , λ · K 0 X = λ · K ( x /squareplus X ( -x ) ) for all x ∈ X . Using axioms ( b 1 ), ( b 2 ) and ( b 4 ) yields λ · K 0 X = ( λ · K x ) /squareplus X ( λ · K ( -x )) = ( λ · K x ) /squareplus X ( λ · K ( -1) · K x ) ) = ( λ · K x ) /squareplus X ( ( -1) · K λ · K x ) ) = ( λ · K x ) /squareplus X ( -( λ · K x ) ) = 0 X . ( b ) We have for all x ∈ X , 0 K · K x = ( 1 K /squareplus ( -1 K ) ) x = (1 K · K x ) /squareplus X ( ( -1 K ) · K x ) = x /squareplus X ( -x ) = 0 X . ( c ) For all x, y ∈ X , ( ( -y ) /squareplus X ( -x ) ) /squareplus X ( x /squareplus X y ) = ( ( -1 K ) · K y /squareplus X ( -1 K ) · x ) /squareplus X ( x /squareplus X y ) = ( ( -1 K ) · ( y /squareplus X x ) ) /squareplus X ( x /squareplus X y ) = -( x /squareplus X y ) /squareplus X ( x /squareplus X y ) = 0 X . ✷

- ( c ) ( ( -y ) /squareplus X ( -x ) ) /squareplus X ( x /squareplus X y ) = 0 X .

Proposition 2.1.8 Let X be an idempotent symmetric space. The intersection of two idempotent symmetric subspaces of X is an idempotent symmetric subspace.

Example 2.1.9 ( R n , /squareplus , · ) is an idempotent symmetric space. Y = { ( x 1 , x 2 , x 3 , x 4 ) ∈ R 4 : x 1 = x 2 , x 3 = x 4 } is an idempotent symmetric subspace of R 4 .

In the following, if K is an idempotent pseudo-field, we define the operations for all x, y ∈ K n and all λ ∈ K as follows:

$$x \mathbb { E } _ { K } \, y = ( x _ { 1 } , \dots , x _ { n } ) \, \mathbb { E } _ { K } \, ( y _ { 1 } , \dots , y _ { n } ) = ( x _ { 1 } \mathbb { E } _ { K } \, y _ { 1 } , \dots , x _ { n } \, \mathbb { E } _ { K } \, y _ { n } )$$

and

$$\lambda \cdot _ { K } x & = \lambda \cdot _ { K } ( x _ { 1 } , \dots , x _ { n } ) = ( \lambda \cdot _ { K } x _ { 1 } , \dots , \lambda \cdot _ { K } x _ { n } ) . \\ \\ ,$$

The two following examples are based on this construction.

Example 2.1.10 ( ˜ M n , ˜ /squareplus , ˜ ⊗ ) is an idempotent symmetric space. Example 2.1.11 ( R -n , /squareplus -1 , · ) is an idempotent symmetric space, with R -n = ( R -1 ) n .

In the next example, it is shown that a similar construction can be applied to certain infinite-dimensional spaces.

Example 2.1.12 Suppose that I is a subset of R and let λ be the Lebesgue measure. Let L p ( λ ) be the set of the real valued maps f : I -→ R such that

$$f \oplus & | 1 , + \infty | . \, \text {Let us consider } t \text { binary operation defined over } L _ { p } ( x ) \, \text {bys.} \\ & f \oplus \mathbb { B } \, g \colon x \mapsto f ( x ) \oplus g ( x ) = \begin{cases} f ( x ) & \text {if } \quad | f ( x ) | \ > \, | f ( y ) | \\ \frac { 1 } { f ( x ) } ( f ( x ) + f ( y ) ) & \text {if } \quad | f ( x ) | \ = \, | f ( y ) | \\ & \text {if } \quad | f ( x ) | \ < \, | f ( y ) | . \end{cases}$$

Remark 2.1.13 Notice that that Examples 2.1.10 and 2.1.11 are constructed from an homeomorphic transformation of ( R n , /squareplus , · ) . It is therefore easy to define two convex structures on ( ˜ M n , ˜ /squareplus , ˜ ⊗ ) and ( R -n , /squareplus -1 , · ) respectively. Namely a subset M of ˜ M is M -convex if for all x, y ∈ M and all t ∈ M , x ˜ /squareplus ty ∈ C . Paralleling [1], a subset C of R -n is inverseB -convex if for all x, y ∈ C and all t ∈ [1 , + ∞ ] , x /squareplus -1 ty ∈ C.

The next result specifically concerns the algebraic structure considered in [8]. However it has some immediate implications for the idempotent symmetric spaces defined in examples 2.1.10 and 2.1.11.

Proposition 2.1.14 For all subsets Y of R n , the following claims are equivalent:

- ( a ) Y is an idempotent symmetric subspace of ( R n , /squareplus , · ) .

$$( b ) \ F o r \ a l l \ ( x _ { 1 } , \dots , x _ { m } ) \in Y ^ { m } \ a n d \ a l l \ t \in \mathbb { R } ^ { m } , \ w e \ h a v e \ \coprod _ { i \in [ m ] } t _ { i } x _ { i } \in Y .$$

Proof: Let us prove that ( a ) implies ( b ). If Y is an idempotent symmetric subspace, this property is true for m = 2. Suppose it is true at rank m and let us prove that it is true at rank m + 1. In other words, assume that for all ( x 1 , ..., x m ) ∈ Y m we have: /squareplus i ∈ [ m ] t i x i ∈ Y for all t ∈ R m , we need to prove that if ( x 1 , ..., x m , x m +1 ) ∈ Y m +1 then for all t ∈ R m +1 we have /squareplus i ∈ [ m +1] t i x i ∈ Y . To establish this property, we use equation (12) which implies that if ( x , ..., x , x ) Y m +1 then, for all t R m +1 , we have

$$\prod _ { i \in [ m + 1 ] } t _ { i } x _ { i } = \prod _ { i \in [ m + 1 ] } \left [ t _ { i } x _ { i } \oplus \left ( \prod _ { j \in [ m + 1 ] \ \{ i \} } t _ { j } x _ { j } \right ) \right ] . \quad ( ^ { * } )$$

1 m m +1 ∈ ∈

For all i , the vectors z i = t i x i /squareplus ( /squareplus j ∈ [ m +1] \{ i } t j x j ) are copositive. Thus the associativity holds true for the m -tuple ( z 1 , ..., z m ). However, since we assume that the property holds true at rank m , z i ∈ Y for all i . Thus we deduce that /squareplus i ∈ [ m +1] t i x i ∈ Y . Therefore, the first part of the proof is established. Since Y is a subset of R n , that is an idempotent symmetric space, the converse inclusion is immediate. ✷

The following property is an immediate consequence.

Proposition 2.1.15 Let Y be an idempotent symmetric subspace of ( R n , /squareplus , · ) . Then Y is idempotent symmetric convex.

## 2.2 Generalized Norms and Ultrametric in Limit.

Let ‖ · ‖ be the Euclidean norm defined for all x ∈ R n as ‖ x ‖ = ( ∑ i ∈ [ n ] x i 2 ) 1 2 . Let d be the Euclidean distance defined for all x, y ∈ R n as d ( x, y ) = ‖ x -y ‖ . For all positive natural numbers p , we introduce the map ‖ · ‖ ϕ p : R n -→ R + defined as

The following properties are immediate: ‖ x ‖ ϕ p = 0 ⇐⇒ x = 0; ‖ αx ‖ ϕ p = | α |‖ x ‖ ϕ p ; ‖ x p + y ‖ ϕ p ≤ ‖ x ‖ ϕ p p + ‖ y ‖ ϕ p . We deduce the following statements:

$$\left | x \right | _ { \varphi _ { p } } & = \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } \left ( \| \phi _ { p } ( x ) \| \right ) . \\ \intertext { l l } \text {miediate} \colon \left \| x \right \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } & = 0 \iff x = 0 ; \left \| \alpha x \right \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } = \left | \alpha \right | \| x \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } ; \left \| x \right \| _ { + } ^ { p } y \right \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } \leq \| x \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } \overset { p } { + }$$

Proposition 2.2.1 For all x, y ∈ R n and all α ∈ R , we have the the following properties:

- ( a ) ‖ x ‖ ∞ = lim p -→∞ ‖ x ‖ ϕ p ( b ) ‖ x /squareplus y ‖ ∞ ≤ max {‖ x ‖ ∞ , ‖ y ‖ ∞ } .

Proof: ( a ) For all x ∈ R n , we have

Proof: For all x ∈ R n , we have

For all i ∈ [ n ] and any natural number p , set z ( p ) i = ∣ ∣ ( x i 2 p +1 -y i 2 p +1 ) 1 2 p +1 ∣ ∣ 2 . For all i , we have: lim p -→∞ z ( p ) i = | x i /squareminus y i | 2 . Since for all p , z ( p ) i ≥ 0, it follows that

$$\| x \| _ { \wp _ { p } } = \left ( \sum _ { i \in [ n ] } | x _ { i } | ^ { 2 ( 2 p + 1 ) } \right ) ^ { \frac { 1 } { 2 ( 2 p + 1 ) } } . \\$$

Taking the limit yields ( a ). ( b ) For all natural numbers p , we have:

$$\| x \, ^ { \, \frac { p } { \, } } y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } \leq \| x \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } \, ^ { \, \frac { p } { \, } } + \| y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } .$$

Suppose that { z ( p ) } p ∈ N is a sequence of R n + which converges to some z ∈ R n + . It follows that we have lim p -→∞ ‖ z ( p ) ‖ ϕ p = ‖ z ‖ ∞ . For all x ∈ R n , let us denote | x | = ( | x 1 | , ..., | x n | ). By definition, we have

$$\| x \, ^ { p } _ { + } y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } = \| | x \, ^ { p } _ { + } y | \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } .$$

Moreover, lim p -→∞ | x p + y | = | x /squareplus y | . It follows that:

$$\lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } \| x ^ { \ p } + y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } = \| | x \mathbb { E } \, y | \| _ { \infty } = \| x \, \mathbb { E } \, y \| _ { \infty } .$$

Since ‖ x ‖ ϕ p ≥ 0 and ‖ y ‖ ϕ p ≥ 0, taking the limit on both sides in ( A ) yields the result. ✷

Recall that, for all α, β ∈ R , we adopt the notation α /squareminus β = α /squareplus ( -β ). Let us consider the map d /squareplus : R n × R n → R + defined as:

$$d _ { \mathbb { E } } ( x , y ) = \max _ { i \in [ n ] } | x _ { i } \, \Box \, y _ { i } |$$

In addition, let us consider the map d ϕ p : R n × R n -→ R + defined by:

$$d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ( x , y ) = \| x \, \frac { p } { } \, y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } .$$

This function is a distance relative to the algebraic structure obtained from the binary operation p +. It defines a metric on any ϕ p -vector space. Note that d ϕ p is also a distance in the usual sense because as p ≥ 1, we have for all z ∈ R n the relation:

$$d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ( x , y ) \leq d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ( x , z ) \stackrel { p } { + } d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ( z , y ) \leq d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ( x , z ) + d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ( z , y ) .$$

Lemma 2.2.2 For all x, y ∈ R n , we have

$$\lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ( x , y ) = d _ { \mathbb { E } } ( x , y ) .$$

$$\| x - y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } = \left ( \sum _ { i \in [ n ] } \left ( \left | ( x _ { i } ^ { 2 p + 1 } - y _ { i } ^ { 2 p + 1 } ) ^ { \frac { 1 } { 2 p + 1 } } \right | ^ { 2 } \right ) ^ { 2 p + 1 } \right ) ^ { \frac { 1 } { 2 ( p + 1 ) } } . \\ \intertext { a n d a n y n a t r a l u n b e r p , s e t z _ { i } ^ { ( p ) } = \left | \left ( x _ { i } ^ { 2 p + 1 } - y _ { i } ^ { 2 p + 1 } \right ) ^ { \frac { 1 } { 2 p + 1 } } \right | ^ { 2 } . \text { For all } $ \real p \in \real p + 1 \right | ^ { 2 } . }$$

$$\lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } \| x - \frac { p } { } \, y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } = \left ( \max _ { i \in [ n ] } | x _ { i } \, \Box \, y _ { i } | ^ { 2 } \right ) ^ { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } = d _ { \mathbb { B } } ( x , y ) . \quad \Box$$

Proposition 2.2.3 The map d /squareplus : R n × R n -→ R + defines an ultrametric on R n . For all x, y, z ∈ R n :

- (1) d /squareplus ( x, y ) = d /squareplus ( y, x ) ;
- (2) d /squareplus ( x, y ) = 0 if and only if x = y ;
- (3) d /squareplus ( x, y ) ≤ max { d /squareplus ( x, z ) , d /squareplus ( z, y ) } .

Proof: (1) is immediate. (2) follows from the fact that for all i we have x i /squareminus y i = 0 ⇐⇒ x i = -y i . (3) For all x, y, z ∈ R n and for all natural numbers p , we have

$$d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ( x ; y ) \leq d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ( x , z ) \stackrel { p } { + } d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ( z , y ) .$$

Since for all x, y ∈ R n we have d ϕ p ( x, y ) ≥ 0 and lim p -→∞ d ϕ p ( x, y ) = d /squareplus ( x, y ), we deduce the result. ✷

Note that any ultrametric defines a metric since the inequality d ( x, y ) ≤ max { d ( x, z ) , d ( y, z ) } implies the triangular inequality d ( x, y ) ≤ d ( x, z ) + d ( y, z ). In particular for all x, y, z ∈ E , at least one of the three equalities d ( x, y ) = d ( y, z ) or d ( x, z ) = d ( y, z ) or d ( x, y ) = d ( z, x ) holds. From the above definition, one can conclude several typical properties of ultrametrics.

Proposition 2.2.4 Let Y be an idempotent symmetric subspace of R n . ( Y, d /squareplus ) is an ultrametric space.

Proof: Since Y is an idempotent symmetric space, the metric d /squareplus is well-defined on Y . From Proposition 2.2.3, the result is immediate. ✷

In the following we say that a sequence { x ( k ) } k ∈ N is Ϝ -convergent to x ∈ R n if lim k -→∞ d /squareplus ( x ( k ) , x ) = 0.

/negationslash

Lemma 2.2.5 Suppose that { x ( k ) } k ∈ N is Ϝ -convergent to x ∈ R n . Let I ( x ) = { i ∈ [ n ] : x i = 0 } . Then, there exists some natural number k 0 such that for all k ≥ k 0 and for all i ∈ I ( x ) we have x ( k ) i = x i . Moreover, for all i / ∈ I ( x ) , lim k -→∞ x ( k ) i = 0 .

/negationslash

Proof: Suppose that lim k -→∞ d /squareplus ( x ( k ) i , x ) = 0. Assume that there exits an increasing subsequence { k q } q ∈ N and some i 0 ∈ I ( x ) such that, for all q , x ( k q ) i = x i and let us show a contradiction. In such a case, for all q ∈ N , we have | x ( k q ) i 0 /squareminus x i 0 | = max {| x ( k q ) i 0 | , | x i 0 |} /negationslash = 0. This implies that

$$d _ { \mathbb { E } } ( x ^ { ( k _ { q } ) } , x ) = \max _ { i \in I ( x ) } \{ | x _ { i } ^ { ( k _ { q } ) } \, \exists \, x _ { i } | \} \geq | x _ { i _ { 0 } } ^ { ( k _ { q } ) } \, \exists \, x _ { i _ { 0 } } | = \max \{ | x _ { i _ { 0 } } ^ { ( k _ { q } ) } | , | x _ { i _ { 0 } } | \} > 0 ,$$

that is a contradiction. Hence, there is some k 0 such that for all k ≥ k 0 and for all i ∈ I ( x ) we have x ( k ) i = x i . If i / ∈ I ( x ) then | x ( k ) i /squareminus x i | = | x ( k ) i /squareminus 0 | = | x ( k ) i | and lim k -→∞ d /squareplus ( x ( k ) , x ) = 0. Therefore, lim k -→∞ x ( k ) i = 0. ✷

It is now interesting to see that the idempotent symmetrized space ( ˜ M n , ˜ /squareplus , ˜ ⊗ ) can also be endowed with a suitable form of ultrametric. Let d ˜ /squareplus : ˜ M n × ˜ M n -→ R ∪ {-∞} defined as:

where for all γ ∈ ˜ M , | γ ∣ ∣ ˜ M = ψ ln ( | ψ exp ( γ ) | ) and, for all i , z i ˜ /squareminus w i = ψ ln ( ψ exp ( z i ) /squareminus ψ exp ( w i ) ) . It is therefore easy to check that: ( i ) for all ( z, w ) ∈ ˜ M n × ˜ M n , d ˜ /squareplus ( z, w ) = -∞ ⇐⇒ z = w ; ( ii ) for all z, t, w ∈ ˜ M n , d ˜ /squareplus ( z, w ) ≤ max { d ˜ /squareplus ( z, t ) , d ˜ /squareplus ( t, w ) } . Therefore, paralleling Proposition 2.2.4, ( ˜ M , ˜ /squareplus , ˜ ⊗ ) can also be endowed with a topological structure. In particular, we can construct a standard ultrametric by defining for all z, w ∈ ˜ M n the distance ¯ d ˜ /squareplus ( z, w ) = ψ exp ( d ˜ /squareplus ( z, w ) ) .

$$d _ { \widetilde { \mathbb { E } } } ( z , w ) = \widetilde { \prod } | z _ { i } \widetilde { \mathbb { W } } _ { i } | _ { \widetilde { M } } , \\ \\ ( y _ { i } , ( | y _ { i } , \ ( \alpha ) | ) Proposition 2.2.6 Let Z be an idempotent symmetric subspace of ˜ M n . Then ( Z, ¯ d ˜ /squareplus ) is an ultrametric space.

## 2.3 Geometry of the Ultrametric Ball

In the following, let B /squareplus ( x, r ) = { z ∈ R n : d /squareplus ( x, z ) &lt;r } and B /squareplus ( x, r ] = { z ∈ R n : d /squareplus ( x, z ) ≤ r } respectively denote the open and closed balls centered at x with radius r .

For all x ∈ R n and all subsets I of [ n ], let us consider the map A x : I -→ R + defined for all i ∈ I by A x ( i ) = | x i | . This map associates to each index i the absolute value of the i -th coordinate of x . For all α ∈ R + , A -1 x ( α ) = { i ∈ I : | x i | = α } .

/negationslash

L ( x, y ) is obtained by dropping from [ n ] all the i 's such that x i = y i . It follows that d /squareplus ( x, y ) = 0 if x = y and

Proposition 2.3.1 Let x ∈ R n . Suppose that A x ( [ n ] ) = ⋃ i ∈ [ n ] A x ( i ) = { α j } j ∈ [ m ] with α j &lt; α j +1 for all j ∈ [ m -1] and α j ≥ 0 for all j .

if x = y . In the next statement we describe the geometry of any ball centered at x ∈ R n .

/negationslash

- ( i ) If x = 0 and A x ( [ n ] ) = { 0 } , then for all α ≥ 0 , B /squareplus (0 , α ] = B ∞ (0 , α ] .
- ( iii ) If α ∈ [ α j , α j +1 [ then z ∈ B /squareplus ( x, α ] if and only if:
- ( ii ) If either α = 0 or α ∈ [0 , α 1 [ then B /squareplus ( x, α ] = { x } .

$$| z _ { k } | \leq \alpha , \ \text {for all } k \in \mathcal { A } _ { x } ^ { - 1 } \left ( [ 0 , \alpha _ { j } ] \right ) \ a n d \, z _ { k } = x _ { k } \text { for all } k \in \mathcal { A } _ { x } ^ { - 1 } \left ( [ \alpha _ { j + 1 } , \alpha _ { m } ] \right ) . \\ ( i v ) \ I f \alpha \geq \alpha _ { m } = \| x \| _ { \infty } \ t h e n \ B _ { \mathbb { E } } ( x , \alpha ) = B _ { \infty } ( 0 , \alpha ) .$$

$$( i v ) \ I f \, \alpha \geq \alpha _ { m } = \| x \| _ { \infty } \ t h e n \ B _ { \mathbb { E } } ( x , \alpha ) = B _ { \infty } ( 0 , \alpha ) .$$

/negationslash

Proof: ( i ) is immediate. ( ii ) If α = 0, then B /squareplus ( x, 0 ] = { x } . If α 1 = 0, the case α &lt; α 1 is then excluded. Suppose that α 1 &gt; 0 . For all z = x , max i ∈ [ n ] | x i /squareplus ( -z i ) | ≥ max i ∈ [ n ] | x i | ≥ α 1 . Therefore α &lt; α 1 implies that B /squareplus ( x, α ] = { x } . ( iii ) Suppose that α ∈ [ α 1 , α 2 ]. If there is some i ∈ A -1 x ( [ α 2 , α m ] ) such that z i = x i , then d /squareplus ( x, z ) ≥ α 2 &gt; α 1 . Therefore, if z ∈ B /squareplus ( x, 0 ] we should have z i = x i for all i ∈ A -1 x ( [ α 2 , α m ] ) . Moreover, for all i ∈ A -1 x ( { α 1 } ) we should have | z i | ≤ α. Let us extend this result to some arbitrary j ≤ m -1. Suppose that α ∈ [ α j , α j +1 ]. If there is some i ∈ A -1 x ( [ α j +1 , α m ] ) such that z i = x i , then d /squareplus ( x, z ) ≥ α j +1 &gt; α j . Therefore, if z ∈ B /squareplus ( x, α ], we should have z i = x i for all i ∈ A -1 x ( [ α j +1 , α m ] ) . Moreover, for all i ∈ A -1 x ( [ α 1 , α j ] ) we should have | z i | ≤ α. ( iv ) Suppose now that α ≥ ‖ x ‖ ∞ . By construction α m = ‖ x ‖ ∞ and z ∈ B /squareplus ( x, α ] if and only if | z i | ≤ ‖ x ‖ ∞ , which ends the proof. ✷

/negationslash

Corollary 2.3.2 For all α ≥ 0 , and all x ∈ R n , B /squareplus ( x, α ] is closed with respect to the norm topology.

$$d _ { \mathbb { E } } ( x , y ) = \max \{ d _ { \mathbb { E } } ( x , z ) , d _ { \mathbb { E } } ( z , y ) \} .$$

Since the converse inequality holds, this completes the proof. ✷

Proposition 2.3.6 Let x, y ∈ R n . If x = -y , then for all z ∈ Co ∞ ( x, y ) \{ x, y } , d /squareplus ( x, y ) = d /squareplus ( x, z ) = d /squareplus ( z, y ) .

Proof: Suppose that z ∈ Co ∞ ( x, y ), in such a case there are some r, s, t, w ∈ [0 , 1] with max { r, s, t, w } = 1 such that z = rx /squareplus sx /squareplus ty /squareplus wy. Since z / ∈ { x, y } we have max { r, s } = max { t, w } = 1, and min { r, s } = max { t, w } = 1, otherwise either z = x or z = y . If r = s = t = w = 1, then z = 0 and the result is immediate. Consequently max { r, s } = max { t, w } = 1, min { r, s } &lt; 1 and min { t, w } &lt; 1. Dropping the symmetric terms, it follows that

$$z & = r x \, \mathbb { E } \, s x \, \mathbb { E } \, t y \, \mathbb { E } \, w y = \min \{ r , s \} x \, \mathbb { E } \, \min \{ t , w \} y . \\ \intertext { t } \mathbb { \ } ( \mathbf a ) \, \{ \mathbf a - ( \mathbf a ) ^ { 2 } \} & = \mathbf a + 1 \, ( \mathbf a ) ^ { 2 } \, \mathbf a - 1 \, ( \mathbf a ) ^ { 2 } \, ( \mathbf a ) ^ { 2 } \, ( \mathbf a ) ^ { 2 } \, ( \mathbf a ) ^ { 2 }$$

Since min { r, s } &lt; 1 and min { t, w } &lt; 1, this implies that d /squareplus ( x, y ) = d /squareplus ( x, z ) = d /squareplus ( z, y ) . ✷

## 3 Some Trigonometric Properties in Limit

In this section, we show that certain properties related to the notion of orthogonality can be derived within the framework of the idempotent and symmetric algebraic structure we consider.

## 3.1 Orthogonality and Pythagorean Properties in Limit

In the following, for all p ∈ N , we say that x, y ∈ R n are ϕ p -orthogonal if:

$$\langle x , y \rangle _ { p } = 0 . \\$$

In addition, we say that x, y ∈ R n are Ϝ -orthogonal if:

$$\langle x , y \rangle _ { \infty } = 0 . \\$$

In the sequel, the earlier results are extended to the general case of a triple [ x, y, z ] of R n . Notice that we have for all natural numbers p

$$\langle x ^ { \frac { p } { 2 } } z , y ^ { \frac { p } { 2 } } z \rangle _ { p } = & \sum _ { i \in [ n ] } x _ { i } y _ { i } + \sum _ { i \in [ n ] } ^ { \varphi _ { p } } \left ( - x _ { i } z _ { i } \right ) ^ { \frac { p } { p } } + \sum _ { i \in [ n ] } ^ { \varphi _ { p } } \left ( - y _ { i } z _ { i } \right ) + \sum _ { i \in [ n ] } ^ { \varphi _ { p } } z _ { i } ^ { 2 } . \\ \intertext { f o r e }$$

Therefore:

$$& \quad \lim _ { p \to \infty } \langle x ^ { \ p } - z , y ^ { \underline { \ p } } - z \rangle _ { p } = F _ { [ 4 n ] } ( x _ { 1 } y _ { 1 } , \dots , x _ { n } y _ { n } , - x _ { 1 } z _ { 1 } , \dots , - x _ { n } z _ { n } , - y _ { 1 } z _ { 1 } , \dots , - y _ { n } z _ { n } , z _ { 1 } ^ { 2 } , \dots , z _ { n } ^ { 2 } ) . \\ & \quad \text {In the following, we introduce the operation } \langle \cdot , \cdot \rangle _ { \mathbb { E } } \colon \mathbb { R } ^ { n } \times \mathbb { R } ^ { n } \times \mathbb { R } ^ { n } \longrightarrow \mathbb { R } \text { defined as} \colon$$

In the following, we introduce the operation 〈 〈· , · , ·〉 〉 /squareplus : R n × R n × R n -→ R defined as:

$$\langle ( x , y , z ) _ { \infty } = \lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } \langle x - z , y - z \rangle _ { p } . \\ \text {all } x , y , z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { n } , \text { we say that } [ x , y , z ] \text { is } F \text {-right-aligned in } z \text { if }$$

In the following, for all x, y, z R n , we say that [ x, y, z ] is Ϝ -right-angled in z if

$$\langle \langle x , y , z \rangle \rangle _ { \infty } = 0 . \\$$

In general, a right-angled triangle is not a Ϝ -right-angled triple. This is shown in the next example.

Example 3.1.1 Let x = (1 , -2 , -1) , y = (2 , 3 , -2) and z = (3 , 2 , -3) . We have x -z = ( -2 , -4 , 2) and y -z = ( -1 , 1 , 1) . We have 〈 x -z, y -z 〉 = 0 . Therefore [ x, y, z ] is a right-angled triangle.However Ϝ [12] (2 , -6 , 2 , 3 , 2 , -3 , -6 , -6 , -6 , 9 , 4 , 9) = 9 = 0 . Hence [ x, y, z ] is not a Ϝ -right-angled triple.

Corollary 3.1.6 Suppose that n = 2 . If [ x, y, 0] is a right-angled triangle in z of R 2 , then [ x, y, 0] is Ϝ -right-angled in 0 and it is ϕ p -right-angled in 0 for all natural numbers p .

The following result is an idempotent and non-associative analogue of the Pythagorean theorem.

$$\sum _ { i \in [ I _ { k } ] } x _ { i } y _ { i } = 0 .$$

Proposition 3.1.7 Let x, y, z ∈ R n , and let [ x, y, z ] is a Ϝ -right-angled triple in z . Then for all p ∈ N :

$$d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ^ { 2 } ( x ; y ) = d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ^ { 2 } ( x , z ) \, \stackrel { p } { + } d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ^ { 2 } ( y , z ) ,$$

$$d _ { \mathbb { E } } ( x , y ) = \max \{ d _ { \mathbb { E } } ( x , z ) , d _ { \mathbb { E } } ( y , z ) \} .$$

Proof: If [ x, y, z ] is a Ϝ -right-angled triple in z then

$$\langle \langle x , y , z \rangle _ { \infty } = 0 .$$

Thus, for all p , 〈 x p -z, y p -z 〉 p = 0. It follows that

$$\langle \phi _ { p } ( x ) - \phi _ { p } ( z ) , \phi _ { p } ( y ) - \phi _ { p } ( z ) \rangle = 0 .$$

$$\| \phi _ { p } ( x ) - \phi _ { p } ( y ) \| ^ { 2 } = \| \phi _ { p } ( x ) - \phi _ { p } ( z ) \| ^ { 2 } + \| \phi _ { p } ( y ) - \phi _ { p } ( z ) \| ^ { 2 } .$$

Hence

We deduce that

$$\left \| \phi _ { p } \left ( \phi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \phi _ { p } ( x ) - \phi _ { p } ( y ) ) \right ) \right \| ^ { 2 } & = \varphi _ { p } \left ( \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \| \phi _ { p } ( \phi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \phi _ { p } ( x ) - \phi _ { p } ( z ) ) \| ^ { 2 } ) + \varphi _ { p } \left ( \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \| \phi _ { p } ( \phi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \phi _ { p } ( y ) - \phi _ { p } ( z ) ) \| ^ { 2 } ) \right ) . \\ \intertext { T h u s \colon } \left \| \phi _ { p } \right \| ^ { 2 } \left ( \phi _ { p } ( x ) - \phi _ { p } ( y ) \right ) & \right \| ^ { 2 } & = \varphi _ { p } \left ( \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \| \phi _ { p } ( \phi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \phi _ { p } ( x ) - \phi _ { p } ( z ) ) \| ^ { 2 } ) + \varphi _ { p } \left ( \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \| \phi _ { p } ( \phi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \phi _ { p } ( y ) - \phi _ { p } ( z ) ) \| ^ { 2 } ) \right ) . \\$$

$$d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ^ { 2 } ( x , y ) = \| x \, \frac { p } { } - y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } ^ { 2 } = \| x \, \frac { p } { } \, z \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } \, + \| y \, \frac { p } { } \, z \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } = d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ^ { 2 } ( x , z ) \, + d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ^ { 2 } ( y , z ) .$$

Thus:

Taking the limit, yields:

$$d _ { \mathbb { E } } ^ { 2 } ( x , y ) = \lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ^ { 2 } ( x , y ) = \lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ^ { 2 } ( x , z ) \stackrel { p } { + } d _ { \varphi _ { p } } ^ { 2 } ( y , z ) = \max \{ d _ { \mathbb { E } } ^ { 2 } ( x , z ) , d _ { \mathbb { E } } ^ { 2 } ( y , z ) \} .$$

The final statement immediately follows. ✷

## 3.2 Cosine, Sine in Limit and some Parametrization of the Unit Square

The purpose of this section is to demonstrate that we can use the limiting properties of standard trigonometric concepts to derive analogues of well-known geometric properties within the framework of the non-associative algebra we are studying. Paralleling the usual definition of a cosine, let us define the ϕ p -pseudo-cosine of the angle between two vectors as:

$$\cos _ { p } ( x , y ) = \frac { \langle x , y \rangle _ { p } } { \| x \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } \| y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } } . \\$$

Taking the limit, we define the pseudo Ϝ -cosine as:

$$\cos _ { \infty } ( x , y ) = \frac { \langle x , y \rangle _ { \infty } } { \| x \| _ { \infty } \| y \| _ { \infty } } . \\ \\$$

It is now natural to define a sine function in limit. In [11] a ϕ p exterior product is defined for all v 1 , v 2 , ..., v n ∈ R n as:

where:

$$\in \mathbb { K } ^ { n } \text { as} \colon & & ( v _ { 1 } \stackrel { p } { \wedge } v _ { 2 } \stackrel { p } { \wedge } \dots \stackrel { p } { \wedge } v _ { n } ) = | v _ { 1 } , v _ { 2 } , \dots , v _ { n } | _ { p } ( e _ { 1 } / \widehat { e } _ { 2 } \wedge \dots \wedge e _ { n } ) ,$$

$$( v _ { 1 } \stackrel { p } { \wedge } v _ { 2 } \stackrel { p } { \wedge } , \dots , \stackrel { p } { \wedge } v _ { n } ) & = \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \phi _ { p } ( v _ { 1 } ) \wedge \phi _ { p } ( v _ { 2 } ) \wedge \dots \wedge \phi _ { p } ( v _ { n } ) \, ) \\ & = \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } \left ( | \phi _ { p } ( v _ { 1 } ) , \phi _ { p } ( v _ { 2 } ) , \dots , \phi _ { p } ( v _ { n } ) | \right ) ( e _ { 1 } \wedge e _ { 2 } \wedge \dots \wedge e _ { n } ) .$$

Taking the limit yields:

$$\wedge \dots \wedge v _ { n } ) & = \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } \left ( \phi _ { p } ( v _ { 1 } ) \wedge \phi _ { p } ( v _ { 2 } ) \wedge \dots \wedge \phi _ { p } ( v _ { n } ) \right ) \\ & = \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } \left ( | \phi _ { p } ( v _ { 1 } ) , \phi _ { p } ( v _ { 2 } ) , \dots , \phi _ { p } ( v _ { n } ) | \right ) ( e _ { 1 } \wedge e _ { 2 } \wedge \dots \wedge e _ { n } ) .$$

$$( v _ { 1 } \stackrel { \infty } { \wedge } v _ { 2 } \stackrel { \infty } { \wedge } \dots \stackrel { \infty } { \wedge } v _ { n } ) = | v _ { 1 } , v _ { 2 } , \dots , v _ { n } | _ { \infty } ( e _ { 1 } \stackrel { \infty } { \wedge } e _ { 2 } \stackrel { \infty } { \wedge } \dots \stackrel { \infty } { \wedge } e _ { n } ) .$$

and

In the case of two vectors the exterior product can be identified to the exterior product and the oriented area of the cross product can be interpreted as the positive area of the parallelogram having x and y as sides:

$$x \wedge y = | x , y | = \| x \| \, \| y \| \sin ( x , y ) .$$

The ϕ p -sine function for two vectors x, y ∈ R 2 is defined as

$$\varphi ^ { 1 } ( x , y ) = \frac { | x , y | _ { p } } { \| x \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } \| y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } } = \frac { \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \varphi _ { p } ( x _ { 1 } ) \varphi _ { p } ( y _ { 2 } ) - \varphi _ { p } ( x _ { 2 } ) \varphi _ { p } ( y _ { 1 } ) ) } { \| x \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } \| y \| _ { \varphi _ { p } } } .$$

Along this line, let us define

## 3.3 Some Formalism on the Complex Scalar Field

$$\text {f} \text {, for an } & \theta \in \mathbb { K } ; \text { we have } \text {from } \text {Benna 5.2.1} \\ & \max \{ | \text {pocs} ( \theta ) | , | \text {psin} ( \theta ) | \} = \max \left \{ | \cos _ { \infty } ( \alpha ^ { - 1 } ( \theta , e _ { 1 } ) | , | \sin _ { \infty } ( \alpha ^ { - 1 } ( \theta ) , e _ { 1 } ) | \right \} = 1 . \\ \intertext { f } 3 . 3 \quad \text {Some Formalism on the Complex Scalar Field }$$

We can continue the previous analogies, this time using complex numbers and studying the relative properties of the unit square. Let us denote C ϕ p (0 , 1] = { x ∈ R n : ‖ x ‖ ϕ p = 1 } . Let us consider the bijective map ϕ /natural : C -→ C defined as:

$$\varphi _ { p } ^ { \natural } ( a + i b ) = \varphi _ { p } ( a ) + i \varphi _ { p } ( b ) .$$

Its reciprocal is the map ϕ /natural -1 : C -→ C defined as ϕ /natural p -1 ( a + i b ) = ϕ -1 p ( a ) + i ϕ -1 p ( b ) . The map ϕ /natural p is homogeneous with respect to the real scalar field, i.e. for all ρ ∈ R , ϕ /natural p ( ρ ( a + i b ) ) = ρ ( ϕ p ( a ) + i ϕ p ( b ) ) = ρϕ /sharp p ( a +i b ) . $$\varphi _ { p } ^ { \natural - 1 } ( z ) = \rho ( \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \cos \theta ) + i \varphi _ { p } ^ { - 1 } ( \sin \theta ) ) . \\ \intertext { u s e f i n e } \rho _ { p } = \rho _ { p } + \frac { 1 } { 2 } | ( 1 - | ( 1 + 2 ) + 1 | ) | ^ { 2 } \intertext { s u n d e f i n e } \intertext { p r } 1 + \frac { 1 } { 2 } | ( 1 + 2 ) + 1 | + 2 | ( 1 + 2 ) | = 0 .$$

$$\text {one} \colon & & z ^ { \ p } \cdot w = \varphi _ { p } ^ { \flat } ^ { \flat - 1 } \left ( \varphi _ { p } ^ { \flat } ( z ) \varphi _ { p } ^ { \flat } ( w ) \right ) \\ \text {and } w = c + i d , \text { then} \colon & &$$

$$z \stackrel { p } { \cdot } w & = ( a b - b d ) + i \left ( a d + b c \right ) . \\ \intertext { l i n i t w h o w } \intertext { o n t h i n t w h o w } \intertext { i n t h e f f } \intertext { o n t h e f f }$$

Along this line, taking the limit with respect to the product topology of R 2 , we define the product:

$$z \otimes w = \lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } z ^ { \ p } \cdot w = ( a b \, \Box \, b d ) + i \left ( a d \, \mathbb { E } \, b c \right ) .$$

Note that this product is equivalent to the matrix product defined in [11] for matrices representing complex numbers. We have for all complex numbers of the form z = a +i b and w = c +i d :

$$\left ( \begin{matrix} a & - b \\ b & a \end{matrix} \right ) \otimes \left ( \begin{matrix} c & - d \\ d & c \end{matrix} \right ) = \left ( \begin{matrix} a c \, \Box b d & - ( d \, \Box b c ) \\ a d \, \Box b c & a c \, \Box b d \end{matrix} \right ) . \\$$

Moreover, for all p ∈ N , and all z = a +i b ∈ C we define the ϕ p -module of z as | z | p = ‖ ( a, b ) ‖ ϕ p . Similarly, | z | ∞ = ‖ ( a, b ) ‖ ∞ = max {| a | , | b |} . In the following, we also extend the operation /squareplus to the complex numbers. For all z = a +i b and w = c +i d , we have :

$$z \oplus w = ( a \oplus c ) + i ( b \oplus d ) .$$

We can then establish the following relations.

## 4 Algebraic Formulation of the Kuratowski-Peano Limit of a Line

## 4.1 Algebraic Formulation of a Line

In this section, we demonstrate that the non-associative algebraic structure we consider allows for establishing an algebraic formulation of any limiting line passing through two points. In the standard convex case it is easy to give an algebraic formulation of a line passing trough two different points. In such a case one can define the line passing trough x and y ( x = y ) as:

/negationslash

$$\mathcal { D } ( x , y ) = \{ t x + s y \, \colon s + t = 1 , s , t \in \mathbb { R } \} .$$

Note that there is no restriction imposing the non-negativity of s and t which limits a line to a line-segment. Paralleling this definition one can define a ϕ p -line as:

$$\mathcal { D } ^ { ( p ) } ( x , y ) = \{ t x + s y \colon s + t = 1 , s , t \in \mathbb { R } \} . \\ \intertext { i n } \left [ 8 ] \text { that the subset } \{ t x \oplus s y \colon \max \{ s , t \} = 1 , s , t \geq 0 \right \} \text { is not path-connected and} \\$$

It was pointed out in [8] that the subset { tx /squareplus sy : max { s, t } = 1 , s, t ≥ 0 } is not path-connected and does not describe, over R n , the convex hull in the limit of { x, y } . To that end it was shown in [8] that Co ∞ ( x, y ) = { tx /squareplus rx /squareplus sy /squareplus wy : max { t, r, s, w } = 1 , t, r, s, w ≥ 0 } . In what follows, we take a parallel approach to give the algebraic description of a line. Let us consider the following subset:

$$\mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } ( x , y ) \coloneqq \left \{ t x \oplus r x \oplus s y \oplus w y \colon t \oplus r \oplus s \oplus w = 1 , t , r , s , w \in \mathbb { R } \right \} . \\ \intertext { a m a 4 . 1 . 1 } \text { For all } x , y \in \mathbb { R } ^ { n } \text { with } x \neq y ,$$

Lemma 4.1.1 For all x, y ∈ R n with x = y ,

/negationslash

$$\mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } ( x , y ) \subset \underset { p \longrightarrow \infty } { L i } \mathcal { D } ^ { ( p ) } ( x , y ) .$$

Proof: Suppose that z ∈ { tx /squareplus rx /squareplus sy /squareplus wy : t /squareplus r /squareplus s /squareplus w = 1 , t, r, s, w ∈ R } . By definition, there exist α 1 , α 2 , β 1 , β 2 ∈ R with α 1 /squareplus α 2 /squareplus β 1 /squareplus β 2 = 1 and such that

$$z = \alpha _ { 1 } x \, \mathbb { E } \, \alpha _ { 2 } x \, \mathbb { E } \, \beta _ { 1 } y \, \mathbb { E } \, \beta _ { 2 } y .$$

Since α 1 /squareplus α 2 /squareplus β 1 /squareplus β 2 = 1, there exists some p 0 ∈ N such that, for all p ≥ p 0 , α 1 p + α 2 p + β 1 p + β 2 = 0. For all p ≥ p 0 , define

/negationslash

By construction, for all p ≥ p 0 , z ( p ) ∈ D ( p ) ( x, y ). Taking the limit on both sides yields:

$$z ^ { ( p ) } = \frac { 1 } { \alpha _ { 1 } \, ^ { \, p } + \alpha _ { 2 } \, ^ { \, p } + \beta _ { 1 } \, ^ { \, p } + \beta _ { 2 } } \, ( \alpha _ { 1 } x + \alpha _ { 2 } x + \beta _ { 1 } y + \beta _ { 2 } y ) . \\ \intertext { z } \intertext { a l l } \intertext { p > p _ { 0 } , \, z ^ { ( p ) } \in \mathcal { P } ^ { ( p ) } ( x , y ) . \, T a k i n g \, t h e \, l i m i t \, o n \, b o t h \, s i d e s \, v i e l d s \colon }$$

$$\lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } z ^ { ( p ) } & = \frac { 1 } { \alpha _ { 1 } \oplus \alpha _ { 2 } \oplus \beta _ { 1 } \oplus \mathbb { B } _ { 2 } } \left ( \alpha _ { 1 } x \oplus \alpha _ { 2 } x \oplus \beta _ { 1 } y \oplus \beta _ { 2 } y \right ) \\ & = \alpha _ { 1 } x \oplus \alpha _ { 2 } x \oplus \beta _ { 1 } y \oplus \beta _ { 2 } y = z .$$

Consequently, z ∈ Li p -→ + ∞ D ( p ) ( x, y ). ✷

Lemma 4.1.2 Let { ( s ( p ) , t ( p ) ) } p ∈ N be sequence of R 2 . Suppose there is an increasing subsequence { p q } q ∈ N of natural numbers such that s ( p q ) p q + t ( p q ) = 1 and lim q -→∞ ( s ( p q ) , t ( p q ) ) = ( s, t ) . We have the following properties:

- ( a ) If max { | s | , | t | } &gt; 1 then s = -t .
- ( c ) If min { | s | , | t | } ≤ 1 then max { s, t } = 1 .
- ( b ) If s = -t then | s | = | t | ≥ 1 .
- ( d ) If s = -t then max { s, t } = 1 .

/negationslash

Proof: ( a ) In such a case if | s | &gt; 1 then t = lim q -→∞ t p q = lim q -→∞ 1 p q -s p q = -s . The proof is symmetrical if t &gt; 1. ( b ) Let us prove that in such a case we have s 1 and t 1. If s &lt; 1 then t = lim t p q =

| | | | ≥ | | ≥ | | q -→∞ lim q -→∞ 1 p q -s p q = 1, which contradicts s = -t . Similarly, if | t | &lt; 1 we have a contradiction. Therefore | s | ≥ 1 and | t | ≥ 1. ( c ) If s = 1, then the conclusion is obvious. If | s | &lt; 1, then t = lim q -→∞ t p q = lim q -→∞ 1 p q -s p q = 1 p q

/negationslash and max { s, t } = 1. To conclude if s = -1, then t = lim q -→∞ t p q = lim q -→∞ 1 + 1 = 1, which ends the proof of ( c ). ( d ) From ( a ), s = -t implies that max { | s | , | t | } ≤ 1. Hence min { | s | , | t | } ≤ 1 and the result is then immediate from ( c ). ✷

The next lemma is a preliminary result to show the upper limit of the sequence {D ( p ) } p ∈ N is identical to D ∞ .

which yields the result. ✷

Proposition 4.1.4 For all x, y ∈ R n with x = y , for all i ∈ [ n ] such that sx i = -ty i .

/negationslash

Proof: Suppose that z ∈ Ls p -→∞ D ( p ) ( x, y ). In such a case there exists an increasing sequence of natural numbers { p k } k ∈ N and sequence { z ( p k ) } k ∈ N with z ( p k ) ∈ D ( p k ) ( x, y ) for all k and lim q -→∞ z ( p k ) = z . This implies that there exists a sequence { s ( p k ) , t ( p k ) } k ∈ N ⊂ R × R such that

$$z ^ { ( p _ { k } ) } = t ^ { ( p _ { k } ) } x + s ^ { ( p _ { k } ) } y ,$$

with s ( p k ) p k + t ( p k ) = 1 for all k . Let us prove that { ( s ( p k ) , t ( p k ) ) } q ∈ N is a bounded sequence. Suppose that this is not the case and let us show a contradiction. Suppose for example that { s ( p k ) } k ∈ N is not bounded. An elementary calculus shows that since t ( p k ) = 1 p k -s ( p k ) . Thus:

$$z ^ { ( p _ { k } ) } = x ^ { \ p _ { k } } + s ^ { ( p _ { k } ) } ( y ^ { \ p _ { k } } - x ) .$$

/negationslash

However lim k -→∞ y p k -x = y /squareminus x . Since x = y , y /squareminus x = 0 and this implies that lim k -→∞ ‖ z ( p q ) ‖ = ∞ that is a contradiction. Consequently { s ( p k ) } k ∈ N is bounded. Since s ( p k ) = 1 p k -t ( p k ) this also implies that { t ( p k ) } k ∈ N is bounded. Therefore, we deduce that the sequence { ( s ( p k ) , t ( p k ) ) } k ∈ N is bounded. Consequently one can extract an increasing subsequence { p k m } m ∈ N such that

/negationslash

$$\lim _ { m \longrightarrow \infty } ( s ^ { ( p _ { k _ { m } } ) } , t ^ { ( p _ { k _ { m } } ) } ) = ( s , p ) .$$

Let us denote I = { i ∈ [ n ] : sx i /squareplus ty i = 0 } . For all i / ∈ I , since sx i = -ty i we have

/negationslash

$$\lim _ { m \longrightarrow \infty } s ^ { ( p _ { k _ { m } } ) } x _ { i } ^ { \ p _ { k _ { m } } } + t ^ { ( p _ { k _ { m } } ) } y _ { i } = s x _ { i } \oplus t y _ { i } ,$$

/negationslash

$$\lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } \mathcal { D } ^ { ( p ) } ( x , y ) = \mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } ( x , y ) .$$

In the next example, it is shown that the formalism described above allows us to define subsets of R n that are unbounded.

Example 4.1.5 Suppose that n = 3 and let us consider the points x = (3 , -2 , 1) and y = (1 , -1 , 1) . Suppose that t = 1 , r = δ = -w and s = 0 where δ is a real number. Clearly, t /squareplus r /squareplus s /squareplus w = 1 /squareplus δ /squareplus ( -δ ) /squareplus 0 = 1 . It follows that

$$\lim _ { | \delta | \longrightarrow \infty } \| z _ { \delta } \| = + \infty .$$

In the next statement, it is shown that, for all x, y ∈ R n with x = y , the line D ∞ ( x, y ) contains two half lines spanned from two specific points. D ∞ ( x, y ) is called the Ϝ -line spanned by x and y .

## 4.3 Parallel Lines

From Proposition 4.2.1, any Ϝ -lines contains two half-lines components. Let D ∞ ( x, y ) and E ∞ ( u, v ) be two Ϝ -lines respectively spanned from x and y and u and v . We say that E ∞ ( u, v ) and D ∞ ( x, y ) are Ϝ -parallel if x /squareminus y ∝ u /squareminus v . Equivalently, this means that x /squareminus y and u /squareminus v are collinear, that is there is some α ∈ R \{ 0 } such that ( x /squareminus y ) = α ( u /squareminus v ). From Proposition 4.2.1, there exist two half lines components E -( u, v ) and E + ( u, v ) of E ∞ ( u, v ) and two half lines components D -( x, y ) and D + ( x, y ) of D ∞ ( x, y ) such that E + ( u, v ) ⊃ ( ⊂ ) D + ( x, y ) and E -( u, v ) ⊃ ( ⊂ ) D -( x, y ).

Fig. 12: Parallel Ϝ -lines.
