Figure 12 depicts the case of two parallel lines. In Figure 13, we consider the case of two secant lines.

Fig. 13: Secant Ϝ -lines.

other

The diagram consists of a semicircle with diameter AB. Point C is located on the semicircle. Point D is the intersection of line CD with the extended line of AB. Point F is the intersection of line DF with line AC.

![Image](C:\Users\KITES\Desktop\Projekt2025\data\OpenRAGBench\out\2401.07317v2_artifacts\image_000012_1493c9c4f0bae33d34f395db0dd64ce63112a9368d089c890ced2b9fb7a73086.png)

Traditional axioms of planar Euclidean geometry postulate that: ( a 1 ) a straight line may be drawn between any two points; ( a 2 ) any terminated straight line may be extended indefinitely; ( a 3 ) a circle may be drawn with any given point as center and any given radius, ( a 4 ) all right angles are equal; ( v ) for any given point not on a given line, there is exactly one line through the point that does not meet the given line.

It is well known that the fifth axiom does not follow from the first four. Clearly, the geometric implications of the algebraic structure proposed in this paper do not conflict with the first 4 axioms. Not surprisingly, this is not the case for the fifth. In particular:

- ( i ) two distinct parallel lines may have an infinity of common points (Figure 12);
- ( ii ) there may exist an infinity of Ϝ -lines passing by two distinct points x and y (Figure 12);
- ( iii ) two secant lines may have an infinity of common points (Figure 13);
- ( iv ) a Ϝ -line may not be spanned by two distinct points it contains (Figure 13).

In the following a system of inequations characterizing a Ϝ -line that passes from two distinct points is provided. This we do by considering the suitable notion of determinant introduced in [10]. The next intermediary result establishes an algebraic description of the limit of a sequence of ϕ p -hyperplanes. The next result was established in [11].

/negationslash

Proposition 4.3.1 Let V be the n × n matrix with v i as i -th column for each i . Let V ( i ) be the matrix obtained from V by replacing line i with the transpose of the unit vector 1 1 n . Suppose that | V | ∞ = 0 . Let { H p ( V ) } p ∈ N be the sequence of ϕ p -hyperplanes passing trough each point v i . Then

$$\lim _ { p \longrightarrow \infty } H _ { p } ( V ) = \left \{ x \in \mathbb { R } ^ { n } \colon \underbar { \bigcup } _ { i \in [ n ] } | V _ { ( i ) } | _ { \infty } x _ { i } \leq | V | _ { \infty } \leq \underbar { \bigcup } _ { i \in [ n ] } | V _ { ( i ) } | _ { \infty } x _ { i } \right \} .$$

Given two distinct points x, y ∈ R 2 , any point z ∈ D ( x, y ) satisfies the relation:

Equivalently, we have

$$\i s \, x , y & \in \mathbb { K } ; \, \text {any point} \, x \not \in D ( x , y ) \ \text {satisfies} \, ( \text {relation} \colon \\ & | z - x , z - y | = \left | \begin{matrix} z _ { 1 } - x _ { 1 } & z _ { 1 } - y _ { 1 } \\ z _ { 2 } - x _ { 2 } & z _ { 2 } - y _ { 2 } \end{matrix} \right | = 0 . \\ & ( x _ { 2 } - y _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } + ( y _ { 1 } - x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } = | x , y | .$$

$$( x _ { 2 } - y _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } + ( y _ { 1 } - x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } & = | x , y | . \\$$

/negationslash

$$\mathbb { D } ^ { \infty } ( x , y ) & = \left \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon ( x _ { 2 } \, \partial _ { y _ { 2 } } ) _ { z _ { 1 } } \, \bar { \cup } ( y _ { 1 } \, \partial x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \, \leq & | x , y | _ { \infty } \leq ( x _ { 2 } \, \partial _ { y _ { 2 } } ) z _ { 1 } \, \stackrel { + } { \cup } ( y _ { 1 } \, \partial x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \right \} . \\ \intertext { I n the following , we show that the equations of two parallel lines in the plane obey algebraic rules }$$

The following proposition establishes an analogous result in the case of the Ϝ -line D ∞ . This algebraic characterization is an immediate consequence of Proposition 4.3.1 (see [11]). Let x, y ∈ R 2 with x = y . Then:

In the following, we show that the equations of two parallel lines in the plane obey algebraic rules analogous to those of the linear case.

Lemma 4.3.2 Let D ∞ and E ∞ be two parallel Ϝ -lines of R 2 . Then there exists a ∈ R 2 , c, d ∈ R such that:

$$\mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } = \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon \langle a , z \rangle _ { \infty } ^ { - } \leq c \leq \langle a , z \rangle _ { \infty } ^ { + } \leq c \}$$

$$\mathcal { E } ^ { \infty } = \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon \langle a , z \rangle _ { \infty } ^ { - } \leq d \leq \langle a , z \rangle _ { \infty } ^ { + } \leq c \} .$$

Proof: Suppose now that D ∞ and E ∞ are two Ϝ -line respectively spanned from x, y and u, v , where x, y, u, v ∈ R 2 . By definition we have:

/negationslash

$$\mathcal { E } ^ { \infty } & = \left \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon ( u _ { 2 } \, \Box v _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } \, \bigcup ( v _ { 1 } \, \Box u _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \leq | u , v | _ { \infty } \leq ( u _ { 2 } \, \Box v _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } \, \bigcup ( v _ { 1 } \, \Box u _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \right \} . \\ \text {If } \mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } \text { and } \mathcal { E } ^ { \infty } \text { are parallel then } ( u \, \Box v ) \, \subset ( x \, \Box y ) . \text { Therefore, there is a real number } \alpha \neq 0 \text { such that}$$

If D ∞ and E ∞ are parallel then ( u /squareminus v ) ∝ ( x /squareminus y ). Therefore, there is a real number α = 0 such that ( u /squareminus v ) = α ( x /squareminus y ). Hence:

$$( u \subseteq 0 ) & \quad \& ( u \subseteq y ) \ \text {R} \ \infty \\ & \quad \& \mathcal { E } ^ { \infty } = \left \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon \alpha ( x _ { 2 } \oplus y _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } \ \bar { \ } \alpha ( y _ { 1 } \oplus x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \leq | u , v | _ { \infty } \leq \alpha ( x _ { 2 } \oplus y _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } \ \stackrel { + } { \wr } \ \alpha ( y _ { 1 } \oplus x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \right \} . \\ \text {Suppose that } \alpha > 0 , \text { then} \quad &$$

Suppose that α &gt; 0, then:

$$\text {Suppose that } & \mathcal { A } \succ 0 ; \text {, the} . \quad \text {and} . \quad \mathcal { A } \supseteq \mathcal { A } ^ { - 1 } | u _ { \ } v | _ { \infty } \leq ( x _ { 1 } \sqcup \mathcal { A } _ { 1 } \sqcup \mathcal { A } _ { 1 } ) z _ { 1 } \supseteq \mathcal { A } ^ { - 1 } | u _ { \ } v | _ { \infty } \leq ( x _ { 2 } \sqcup \mathcal { A } _ { 2 } ) z _ { 2 } \underbrace { \ + } ( y _ { 1 } \sqcup x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \Big \} . \\ & \mathcal { E } ^ { \infty } = \Big \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon ( x _ { 2 } \sqcup y _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } \sqcup ( y _ { 1 } \sqcup x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \supseteq \mathcal { A } ^ { - 1 } | u _ { \ } v | _ { \infty } \leq ( x _ { 2 } \sqcup y _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } \supseteq ( y _ { 1 } \sqcup x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \Big \} . \\ \intertext { We deduce the result taking } & a = x \sqcup y , \, c = | x , y | _ { \infty } , \, \text {and} \, d = \alpha ^ { - 1 } | u , v | _ { \infty } \text {. $$\mathcal { E } ^ { \infty } = & \left \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon ( x _ { 2 } \, \partial y _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } \, \bar { \cup } ( y _ { 1 } \, \partial x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \, \right \} \alpha ^ { - 1 } | u , v | _ { \infty } \geq ( x _ { 2 } \, \partial y _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } \, \underbrace { \cup } ( y _ { 1 } \, \partial x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \right \} . \\ \intertext { S i n c . ( r o . \, \partial y _ { 2 } ) _ { z _ { 1 } } \, \bar { \cup } ( y _ { 1 } \, \partial x _ { 1 } ) = & \, - \left ( ( y _ { 0 } \, \partial x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 1 } ^ { + } \, \underset { \cdot } { \underset { z _ { 1 } } { \underset { x _ { 1 } } { \cup } } } \right ) \, \text {and} \, ( x _ { 0 } \, \partial x _ { 1 } \, \cup \, z _ { 1 } ^ { + } \, ( x _ { 1 } \, \partial y _ { 1 } ) ) = & \, - \left ( ( y _ { 0 } \, \partial x _ { 1 } ) z _ { 1 } \, \bar { \cup } \right )$$

Since ( x 2 /squareminus y 2 ) z 1 -/slurbelow ( y 1 /squareminus x 1 ) = -( ( y 2 /squareminus x 2 ) z 1 + /slurbelow ( x 1 /squareminus y 1 ) ) and ( x 2 /squareminus y 2 ) z 1 + /slurbelow ( y 1 /squareminus x 1 ) = -( ( y 2 /squareminus x 2 ) z 1 -/slurbelow ( x 1 /squareminus y 1 ) ) we deduce that:

and

$$\mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } = \left \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon ( y _ { 2 } \, \Box \, x _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } \, \bigcup \, ( x _ { 1 } \, \Box \, y _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \leq | y , x | _ { \infty } \leq ( y _ { 2 } \, \Box \, x _ { 2 } ) z _ { 1 } \, \bigcup \, ( x _ { 1 } \, \Box \, y _ { 1 } ) z _ { 2 } \right \} . \\ \\ \text {Hence} \, \text {taking} \, \varrho \, \equiv \, y \, \Box \, x _ { 1 } \, \colon \, c \equiv | y _ { 1 } x _ { 1 } \, \Box \, \text {and} \, d \equiv | \alpha | ^ { - 1 } | y _ { 1 } | y _ { 1 } \, \text {olds the result} \, \Box \,$$

Example 4.3.3 Suppose that x = (3 , 1) , y = (1 , -2) , u = ( -2 , 4) and v = ( -6 , 1) . We have:

$$x \, \Box \, y & = \left ( \begin{matrix} 3 \, \Box \, 1 \\ 1 \, \Box \, ( - 2 ) \end{matrix} \right ) = \left ( \begin{matrix} 3 \\ 2 \end{matrix} \right ) \quad a n d \quad u \, \Box \, v = \left ( \begin{matrix} - 2 \, \Box \, ( - 6 ) \\ 4 \, \Box \, 1 \end{matrix} \right ) = \left ( \begin{matrix} 6 \\ 4 \end{matrix} \right ) .$$

It follows that u /squareminus v = 2( x /squareminus y ) . Moreover,

$$| x , y | _ { \infty } = 3 \cdot ( - 2 ) \, \Box \, 1 \cdot 1 = - 6 \quad a n d \quad | u , v | _ { \infty } = ( - 2 ) \cdot 1 \, \Box \, ( - 6 ) \cdot 4 = 2 4 .$$

Therefore:

Hence:

$$\mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } ( x , y ) = \left \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon \left ( 1 \, \exists \, ( - 2 ) \right ) z _ { 1 } \, \subsetneq ( 1 \, \exists \, 3 ) z _ { 2 } \leq - 6 \leq ( 1 \, \exists \, ( - 2 ) ) z _ { 1 } \overset { + } { \sim } ( 1 \, \exists \, 3 ) z _ { 2 } \right \} . \\ \\ H e n c e \colon \quad & \left ( \begin{array} { c c } 0 \\ 1 \end{array} \right )$$

In addition we have:

$$\mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } ( x , y ) \equiv \left \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon 2 z _ { 1 } \ \bar { \cup } \left ( \cdot - 3 \right ) z _ { 2 } \leq - 6 \leq 2 z _ { 1 } \ \underbrace { + } \left ( \cdot - 3 \right ) z _ { 2 } \right \} .$$

$$\mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } ( u , v ) & = \left \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon ( 4 \, \Box ( - 1 ) ) z _ { 1 } \subseteq ( ( - 6 ) \, \Box ( - 2 ) ) z _ { 2 } \leq - 6 \leq ( 4 \, \Box ( - 1 ) ) z _ { 1 } \overset { + } { \bigcup } ( ( - 6 ) \, \Box ( - 2 ) ) z _ { 2 } \right \} . \\ \\ H e n c e \colon & \quad & \intertext { \mathcal { D } } H e n c e \colon & \quad & \intertext { o f } & \intertext { s u n t } H e n c e \colon & & \intertext { s u n t }$$

Hence:

Equivalently, by changing the sign and simplifying by 2:

$$\mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } ( u , v ) = \left \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon ( - 4 ) z _ { 1 } \smile \bar { 6 } z _ { 2 } \leq 2 4 \leq ( - 4 ) z _ { 1 } \smile \bar { 6 } z _ { 2 } \right \} . \\ \intertext { b y \ c h a n g i n g \ t h e \ s i g n \ a n d \ s i m p l i f y i n g \ b y \ 2 \colon }$$

$$\mathcal { D } ^ { \infty } ( u , v ) = \left \{ z \in \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 } \colon 2 z _ { 1 } \smile ( - 3 ) z _ { 2 } \leq - 1 2 \leq 2 z _ { 1 } \smile ( - 3 ) z _ { 2 } \right \} . \\ \intertext { i c e t h a t h e c o f f i c i e n t s } \ m u l t i p l { i n g } \ z _ { 1 } \ a n d \ z _ { 2 } \ a r e \ t h e \ s a m e \ t h e \ d i f f e r e n c e \ r e l a t i n g$$

We can notice that the coefficients multiplying z 1 and z 2 are the same the difference relating only to the constant term.

## Conclusion

In this article, we have explored certain geometric properties emerging from the deformation of a vector space structure defined over a scalar field. In particular, we have focused on notions of lines that can be seen as special limits of linear varieties. For future research, it would be valuable to investigate the connections between these notions and those typically derived from Max-Plus algebras, which are traditionally used in the formalism of tropical mathematics, as initiated in [29] and [27].
